Disbeliever's Oath: No Expiation Upon Breaking
Ruling on disbeliever's oath: no expiation required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a disbeliever's oath and explain why expiation is not required based on the hadith.

Core Idea
A disbeliever's oath is legally invalid; breaking it incurs no expiation even after embracing Islam.

This lesson examines the unique Hanafi position regarding oaths sworn by a disbeliever. While a Muslim's broken oath requires expiation (kaffarah), the same does not apply to a disbeliever. The key reason is that a valid oath must be sworn by Allah, and the disbeliever's actions are not legally recognized acts of worship. We will explore the matn from al-Quduri and its supporting hadith.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Read the matn: 'If a disbeliever swears an oath, then breaks it during disbelief or after his Islam, there is no expiation upon him.' Identify the core legal outcome.
2
Identify the timing
Note that the ruling applies whether the oath is broken during disbelief or after embracing Islam. The timing does not affect the absence of expiation.
3
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith: 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or remain silent.' Recognize that this hadith is the evidentiary basis.
Muttafaq alayh: Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith implies that a valid oath must be by Allah. A disbeliever's oath is not a valid swearing by Allah because his acts of worship are invalid. Thus, the oath is void and expiation is not triggered.
5
Note cross-madhhab status
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih. The Shafi'i school holds that if a kafir swears by Allah, the oath is valid after conversion. Understand the source of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ حَلَفَ الْكَافِرُ ثُمَّ حَنِثَ فِي حَالِ الْكُفْرِ أَوْ بَعْدَ إِسْلَامِهِ فَلَا حِنْتَ عَلَيْهِ. دَليلُهُ: عَنْ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ بْنِ عُمَرَ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمَا أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «مَنْ كَانَ حَالِفًا فَلْيَحْلِفْ بِاللَّهِ أَوْ لِيَصْمُتْ.» (رَوَاهُ الْبُخَارِيُّ وَمُسْلِمٌ)
And if a disbeliever swears an oath, then breaks it during disbelief or after his Islam, then there is no expiation upon him. Its proof: From Abdullah ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or else remain silent.' (Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim)
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This ruling addresses the oath of a disbeliever (kafir). Since the disbeliever's actions are not valid worship acts according to Hanafi fiqh, his oath is not legally binding. Therefore, even if he breaks it, whether before or after embracing Islam, he is not required to perform expiation. The hadith indicates that a valid oath must be by Allah; a disbeliever may not swear by Allah in a valid manner, so his oath is ineffective. After Islam, past oaths are not binding. Note: This applies to oaths that would require expiation if broken by a Muslim.

Key Terms
كافر (kafir): One who disbelieves in Islam or rejects its fundamental tenets.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a disbeliever swears an oath and later breaks it after embracing Islam, what is required?
A) He must expiate because he is now a Muslim.
B) He must repent but no expiation.
C) No expiation is required.
D) He must repeat the oath properly.

Key Takeaway
A disbeliever's broken oath never requires expiation, even after conversion, because his oath was never legally valid.

Declaring Halal as Haram: Kaffarah Yamin Required
Ruling and proof on declaring halal as haram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on tahrim (declaring halal as haram) and cite the Quranic proof and the hadith of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
Saying 'this is haram for me' does not make it haram, but using it requires kaffarah yamin.

This lesson addresses a common scenario: when a person verbally declares something lawful as forbidden upon themselves. In the Hanafi school, such a declaration does not alter the thing's legal status. However, it is legally treated as an oath (yamin). Consequently, if the person uses or enjoys that thing, they must pay kaffarah yamin. We will examine the ruling, its evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah, and how it differs from other madhahib.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify what the matn says: If someone declares something haram that they own, it does not become haram, but if they use it, they owe kaffarah yamin.
2
Identify the proof
Locate the primary proof: Quran 66:1-2, where Allah tells the Prophet that He has ordained 'tahlilat aymanikum' (dissolution of oaths), and the saying of Ibn Abbas that such a statement requires expiation.
The verse shows that tahrim is treated as an oath.
3
Analyze the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the verse proves the ruling: The phrase 'tahlilat aymanikum' indicates that the statement of tahrim is considered a kind of oath, thus breaking it requires kaffarah.
The Hanafi school takes this as the basis for equating tahrim with yamin.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Compare with the Shafi'i view: They treat tahrim of a spouse as zihar (requiring kaffarah of zihar) and otherwise not binding.
This difference has practical consequences in family law.
5
Apply the ruling
Practice: Given a scenario like 'Zayd says, "This pomegranate is haram for me," then eats it,' determine that he must pay kaffarah yamin.
The kaffarah is feeding/clothing ten poor, freeing a slave, or fasting three days, in that order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَرَّمَ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ شَيْئًا مِمَّا يَمْلِكُهُ لَمْ يَصِرْ مُحَرَّمًا عَلَيْهِ وَعَلَيْهِ إِنْ اسْتَبَاحَهُ كَفَّارَةُ يَمِينٍ. دَليلُهُ: قَالَ تَعَالَى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللَّهُ لَكَ تَبْتَغِي مَرْضَاتَ أَزْوَاجِكَ وَاللَّهُ غَفُورٌ رَّحِيمٌ * قَدْ فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَانِكُمْ﴾ [التحريم: 1-2]. وَعَنِ ابْنِ عَبَّاسٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمَا قَالَ فِي الْحَرَامِ: يُكَفَّرُ.
And whoever declares forbidden upon himself something that he owns, it does not become forbidden upon him, and if he makes use of it, he must pay expiation of an oath. Its proof: Allah said: 'O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you, seeking the approval of your wives? And Allah is Forgiving and Merciful. Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths.' [Quran 66:1-2]. And from Ibn Abbas who said concerning 'haram': it is expiated.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This is a key Hanafi ruling on the issue of 'tahrim' (declaring something haram). If a person says, 'This food is haram for me,' or 'My wife is haram for me,' it does not actually become haram. However, if he proceeds to use or enjoy that thing, he must pay kaffarah yamin (expiation of an oath). This is because the statement is treated as an oath (yamin) according to the Hanafi school, based on the Quranic verse that considers tahrim as a form of oath. The proof includes Ibn Abbas's saying that such a statement requires expiation. Note: This applies only to that which one owns; one cannot declare haram what is not his to begin with.

Key Terms
تحريم (tahrim): Declaring something lawful as forbidden upon oneself.
كفارة يمين (kaffarah yamin): Expiation for breaking an oath: feeding/clothing ten poor, freeing a slave, or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Hanafi fiqh, if someone says 'This food is haram for me' and then eats it, what is required?
A) Nothing, since the food is not actually haram.
B) He must pay kaffarah yamin.
C) He must repent but no kaffarah.
D) He must divorce his wife if the statement was about her.

Key Takeaway
Declaring halal as haram is treated as an oath; using the item requires kaffarah yamin.

Ruling on Saying 'All Halal is Haram'
Ruling on the legal effect of 'كل حلال علي حرام'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'كل حلال علي حرام' and explain why it is treated as an oath, not as a divorce.

Core Idea
Saying 'all halal is haram' is an oath, not a divorce — it requires kaffarah, not talaq.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses a common verbal declaration: a person says 'every lawful thing is forbidden to me.' The Hanafi school clarifies that this does not make halal things actually haram, nor does it divorce a spouse. Instead, it is treated as an oath (yamin) binding upon food and drink unless the person intended something else. The ruling is supported by the practice of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The phrase 'كل حلال علي حرام'. The ruling is that it is an oath pertaining to food and drink, as per the matn: فهو على الطعام والشراب.
The phrase does not change the legal status of things.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? Narrations from Abu Bakr, Ibn Mas'ud, al-Sha'bi, and 'Ali (radiyallahu 'anhum). These are qawl al-sahabi and qawl al-tabi'i.
The dalil is zanni al-thubut but clear in dalalah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the dalil prove the ruling? The Companions explicitly state that saying 'هي علي حرام' is an oath (yamin) and requires kaffarah, not divorce. This establishes the general rule.
The wording is treated as an oath by default.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any scholar disagree? 'Ali (radiyallahu 'anhu) held that it makes the wife prohibited until she marries another, but also requires kaffarah.
The Hanafi school follows the majority of Companions.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The default ruling (oath regarding food and drink) is mufta bihi, based on the stronger evidence from Abu Bakr, Ibn Mas'ud, and al-Sha'bi.
Intention can shift the ruling, but that is exceptional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's matn states the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'كل حلال علي حرام' (all that is lawful for me is forbidden). The default ruling is that it is considered an oath (yamin) regarding food and drink, meaning it triggers the expiation of an oath (kaffarah yamin) if broken, and does not result in divorce or actual prohibition of the wife unless he intended that. The commentary provides proofs from the Companions and Successors: Abu Bakr and Ibn Mas'ud held it is an oath, not divorce; al-Sha'bi said it does not cause divorce or prohibition but requires kaffarah; 'Ali had a different view that it makes the wife prohibited until she marries another, but the Hanafi position follows the majority that it is an oath.

Key Terms
كل حلال علي حرام (kullu halalin 'alayya haram): A verbal expression by which one declares lawful things to be forbidden; legally treated as an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person says 'كل حلال علي حرام' without any intention, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) It is considered a divorce (talaq)
B) It is considered an oath (yamin) regarding food and drink
C) It is considered a declaration of zihar
D) It is considered a vow (nadhr)

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'كل حلال علي حرام' is treated as an oath regarding food and drink, requiring kaffarah if broken, and does not affect marital status unless specific intention of talaq is made.

Obligation of Vows and Expiation for Unspecified Vows
Rulings and proofs for vows

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on unconditional and conditional vows, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding conditional unspecified vows, and apply the rule that kaffarah yamin suffices for such vows.

Core Idea
Unconditional vows must be fulfilled; conditional vows left unspecified are expiated by kaffarah yamin.

Vows (nadhur) are serious commitments in Islam. The Hanafi school distinguishes between unconditional vows that must be fulfilled and conditional vows that, if left unspecified, are treated like oaths. This lesson explores the proofs from the Quran and Hadith, and examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Hanifa retracted his earlier view to agree with Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of vows
Read the passage and note that vows are either unconditional (munjaz) or conditional (mu'allaq). The unconditional must be fulfilled; the conditional may require expiation if not specified.
The Arabic terms are 'مطلق' (unconditional) and 'معلق' (conditional).
2
Locate the primary proofs
The passage cites Quran 22:29 and 2:270, and a hadith from Aisha (Bukhari). Identify how each proves the obligation to fulfill vows of obedience.
The imperative 'ليوفوا' in 22:29 indicates obligation (wujub) in Hanafi usul.
3
Understand the hadith on unspecified vows
The hadith from Ibn Abbas states that an unspecified vow, a vow to sin, or a vow beyond one's capacity all require kaffarah yamin. This is the basis for treating certain conditional vows as oaths.
This hadith is from Abu Dawud and Ibn Majah; it supports the final Hanafi position.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Hanifa initially held a different view but retracted, adopting Muhammad al-Shaybani's position. The operative ruling (mufta bihi) is that kaffarah yamin suffices for conditional vows that are unspecified or impossible.
The text says 'وهو قول محمد' (this is the saying of Muhammad).
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a person who says: 'If I pass the exam, I owe a Hajj.' According to the Hanafi school, this conditional vow is treated as an oath; upon fulfillment of the condition, kaffarah yamin is required, not Hajj.
This is the practical consequence of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووجه : (1) الآية لثبوت ومن نذر نذرا مطلقا فعليه الوفاء به (ثم ليقضوا تفثهم وليوفوا نذورهم وليطوفوا بالبيت العتيق) (سورة الحج، 22 آيت نمبر 29) وجه: (2) الحديث لثبوت ومن نذر ندرا مطلقا فعليه الوفاء به عن عائشة، عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «من نذر أن يطيع الله فليطعه، ومن نذر أن يعصيه فلا يعصه.» (صحيح البخاري، باب النذر في الطاعة، غير 6696) وجه: (3) الآية لثبوت ومن نذر نذرا مطلقا فعليه الوفاء به (وما أنفقتم من نفقة أو نذرتم من نذر فإن الله يعلمه وما للظالمين من أنصار) (سورة البقرة، 2 أيت، نمبر 270) أصول: نذر کی دو صورتیں ہیں اندر مطلق ۲ نذر معلق، نذ کا پورا کرنا یمین کی طرح لازم ہے ورنہ کفارہ ہے۔ وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه رجع عن ذلك وقال إذا قال: إن فعلت كذا فعلي حجة أو صوم سنة أو صدقة ما أملك أجزأه عن ذلك كفارة يمين وهو قول محمد. وجه : (1) الحديث لثبوت وروي عن أبي حنيفة أنه رجع عن ذلك عن ابن عباس، أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «من نذر ندرا لم يسمه فكفارته كفارة يمين، ومن نذر نذرا في معصية فكفارته كفارة يمين، ومن نذر ندرا لا يطيقه فكفارته كفارة يمين، ومن نذر ندرا أطاقه فليف به» (أبو داود شريف، باب من نذر ندرا لا يطيقه، نمبر 3322/ ابن ماجه، من نذر ندرا ولم يسمه، نمبر 2128)
And proof from the verse: 'Then let them complete their rites and fulfill their vows and circumambulate the Ancient House' (22:29). Proof from hadith: 'Whoever vows to obey Allah, let him obey Him...' Proof from verse: 'Whatever expenditure you make or vow you vow, indeed Allah knows it.' Principles: There are two types of vows: unconditional and conditional. Fulfilling a vow is binding like an oath, otherwise expiation is required. Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage deals with the rulings of vows (nadhur). The matn states that an unconditional vow must be fulfilled. The commentary provides Quranic and hadith proofs. Then it discusses a specific type of conditional vow: if someone says 'If I do X, then I owe Hajj, etc.', the Hanafi position (after Abu Hanifa's retraction, now agreeing with Muhammad) is that this is treated as an oath, and kaffarah yamin suffices instead of performing the specified act. The hadith from Ibn Abbas supports this: an unspecified vow is expiated by kaffarah yamin. The Urdu summarises: two types of vows, fulfilling is obligatory like an oath, otherwise expiation.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship or charity.
كفارة يمين (kaffarah al-yamin): Expiation for breaking an oath: feeding ten poor, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He must do nothing because it was a conditional vow
D) He must fast three days

Key Takeaway
Unconditional vows are wajib to fulfill; conditional vows that are unspecified or impossible require kaffarah yamin, following the final Hanafi position.

Oath on 'Bayt' Does Not Include Places of Worship
Ruling on oath not to enter a house – places of worship excluded

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that an oath not to enter a 'bayt' is not broken by entering a place of worship, and apply the same principle to analogous cases using customary understanding.

Core Idea
A mosque is not a 'bayt' in the oath's sense — custom governs the scope of an oath.

In the Hanafi school, the interpretation of oaths follows the customary usage of the swearer. A common pitfall is assuming that any building, especially a religious one, counts as a 'house' under an oath. This ruling from Al-Quduri clarifies that places of worship are not considered 'bayt' in the ordinary sense, so entering them does not break an oath not to enter a house.

Guided Path
1
Identify the oath's subject
The person swears 'not to enter a house' (bayt). Determine the category: a dwelling where one lives.
The Arabic word bayt in everyday usage refers to a residential home.
2
Define the exceptions
The text lists the Kaaba, mosque, synagogue, and church, which are places of worship. Recognize that they are not called 'bayt' in customary speech.
Even the Kaaba, which is called 'Bayt Allah', is not a dwelling house.
3
Apply the rule
Since the places entered are not of the same category as the oath's subject, the oath is not violated.
The ruling: 'lam yahnith' (he is not an oath-breaker).
4
Generalize the principle
The underlying principle: oaths are interpreted according to the customary meaning of the words used, not their literal or metaphorical extensions.
This is consistent with the Hanafi reliance on 'urf (custom) in language.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حلف لا يدخل بيتا فدخل الكعبة أو المسجد أو البيعة أو الكنيسة لم يحنث
And whoever swears not to enter a house, and then enters the Kaaba, or a mosque, or a synagogue, or a church, he is not guilty of breaking the oath.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This ruling from Al-Quduri states that if a person swears an oath not to enter a 'bayt' (house), and then enters the Kaaba, a mosque, a synagogue, or a church, he does not break his oath. The reason is that these places are not called 'bayt' in the customary sense of a dwelling house. The Urdu commentary adds: 'The principle is that if the thing sworn about is not found, then he is not a hanith (oath-breaker). For example, if he swears not to enter a house and enters the Kaaba or a mosque, he is not a hanith.'

Key Terms
بيت (bayt): A dwelling house, not including places of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He has broken his oath and must pay kaffarah
B) He has not broken his oath
C) It depends on his intention
D) He must repeat the oath

Key Takeaway
An oath on entering a house is interpreted according to customary understanding, and places of worship are not classified as houses.

Principle of Customary Usage in Oaths
Series of rulings on oath-breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of customary usage ('urf) to determine when an oath is broken in various scenarios.

Core Idea
In oaths, the customary meaning of words governs, even if the literal meaning differs.

Oaths are serious legal commitments. But when does speaking a word break an oath? The Hanafi school, as presented in this passage, relies on how people customarily understand words, not their strict dictionary definitions. This lesson walks through several examples from the Quduri text to show how 'urf determines hanth (breaking) or lack thereof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sworn object
Read the first part of each ruling to identify what exactly was sworn against (e.g., wheat, flour, speaking to a person).
The object may be general or specific.
2
Determine the customary meaning
Consider how people customarily understand the term in daily life. For example, 'bread' is not 'wheat' in custom.
Apply the principle from the end of the text: 'These types are based on customary usage.'
3
Check whether the action falls under that meaning
Analyze if the actual action (e.g., eating bread, sitting on a rug) falls under the customary scope of the sworn term.
If the action does not customarily belong, no hanth.
4
Note exceptions and special cases
Some actions count as hanth even if not identical (e.g., eating bread made from sworn flour, because flour and bread are customarily the same substance).
Agency and location also affect the ruling.
5
Apply to the final principle
Conclude that all these rulings uphold the meaning that custom ('urf) determines the scope of the oath.
This is a key principle in Hanafi fiqh of oaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكفاره
١ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هَذِهِ الْحِنْطَةِ فَأَكَلَ مِنْ خَبْزِهَا لَمْ يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ مِنْ هَذَا الدَّقِيقِ فَأَكَلَ مِنْ خُبْزِهِ حَنِثَ وَلَوْ اسْتَفَهُ كَمَا هُوَ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لا يُكَلِّمُ فَلَانًا فَكَلَّمَهُ وَهُوَ بِحَيْثُ يَسْمَعُ إِلَّا أَنَّهُ نَائِمٌ حَنِثَ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُهُ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِهِ فَأَذِنَ لَهُ وَلَمْ يَعْلَمْ بِإِذْنِهِ حَتَّى كَلَّمَهُ
19 وَإِذَا اسْتَحْلَفَ الْوَالِي رَجُلًا لِيُعْلِمَهُ بِكُلِ دَاعِرٍ خَبِيثٍ دَخَلَ الْبَلَدَ فَهُوَ عَلَى حَالِ وِلَايَتِهِ خَاصَّةً وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَرْكَبُ دَابَّةَ فُلَانٍ فَرَكِبَ دَابَّةَ عَبْدِهِ لَمْ يَحْنَثْ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَدْخُلُ هَذِهِ الدَّارَ فَوَقَفَ عَلَى سَطْحِهَا أَوْ دَخَلَ دِهْلِيزَهَا حَنِثَ فَإِنْ وَقَفَ عَلَى طَاقِ الْبَابِ بِحَيْثُ إِذَا غُلِقَ الْبَابُ كَانَ خَارِجًا لَمْ يَحْنَتْ
٢٠ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الشَّوَاءَ فَهُوَ عَلَى اللَّحْمِ دُونَ الْبَاذِنْجَانِ وَالْجْزَرِ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الطَّبِيحَ فَهُوَ عَلَى مَا يُطْبَخُ مِنْ اللَّحْمِ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ الرُّءُوسَ فَيَمِينُهُ عَلَى مَا يُكْبَسُ فِي التَّنَانِيرِ وَيُبَاعِ فِي الْمِصْرِ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَأْكُلُ خُبْزًا فَيَمِينُهُ عَلَى مَا يَعْتَادُ أَهْلُ الْمِصْرِ أَكْلَهُ خُبْزًا فَإِنْ أَكَلَ الْقَطَائِفَ أَوْ خُبْزَ الْأُرْزِ بِالْعِرَاقِ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ
ال وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَبِيعُ وَلَا يَشْتَرِي وَلَا يُؤَجِّرُ فَوَكَّلَ مَنْ فَعَلَ ذَلِكَ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَتَزَوَّجُ أَوْ لا يُطَلِقُ أَوْ لا يُعْتِقُ فَوَكَّلَ مَنْ فَعَلَ ذَلِكَ حَنِثَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَجْلِسُ عَلَى الْأَرْضِ فَجَلَسَ عَلَى بِسَاطِ أَوْ حَصِيرٍ لَمْ يَحْنَثْ ۲۲ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَجْلِسُ عَلَى سَرِيرٍ فَجَلَسَ عَلَى سَرِيرٍ فَوْقَهُ بِسَاطٌ حَنِثَ وَإِنْ جَعَلَ فَوْقَهُ سَرِيرًا آخَرَ فَجَلَسَ عَلَيْهِ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَنَامُ عَلَى فِرَاشٍ فَنَامَ عَلَيْهِ وَفَوْقَهُ قِرَامٌ حَنِثَ فَإِنْ جَعَلَ فَوْقَهُ فِرَاشًا آخَرَ لَمْ يَحْنَتْ
۲۰ اصول : ان قسموں کا مدار محاورات پر ہے، لہذا محاورات میں دیکھیں کیا مراد ہے اگر چہ معنی عام ہوں۔
Chapter on Expiation
1. Whoever swears not to eat from this wheat, then eats from its bread, does not break his oath. If he swears not to eat from this flour, then eats from its bread, he breaks his oath. If he sniffs it as is, he does not break his oath. If he swears not to speak to so-and-so, and speaks to him while he is in a position to hear, except that he is asleep, he breaks his oath. If he swears not to speak to him except with his permission, and he permits him, but he does not know of the permission until he speaks, ... [he breaks?]. 
19. If a governor makes a man swear to inform him of every wicked corrupt person who enters the city, it applies specifically to his tenure. Whoever swears not to ride the mount of so-and-so, and rides the mount of his slave, does not break his oath. Whoever swears not to enter this house, and stands on its roof or enters its entrance, breaks his oath. If he stands on the arch of the door such that when the door is closed he is outside, he does not break his oath.
20. Whoever swears not to eat 'shawa' (roasted meat), it applies to meat, not eggplant or carrots. If he swears not to eat 'tabikh', it applies to what is cooked from meat. If he swears not to eat 'ru'us' (heads), his oath applies to what is baked in ovens and sold in the city. If he swears not to eat bread, his oath applies to what the people of the city customarily eat as bread. If he eats 'qata'if' or rice bread in Iraq, he does not break his oath.
And whoever swears not to sell, buy, or lease, and appoints an agent to do that, does not break his oath. If he swears not to marry, not to divorce, or not to free, and appoints an agent to do that, he breaks his oath. Whoever swears not to sit on the ground, and sits on a rug or mat, does not break his oath. 22. Whoever swears not to sit on a bed, and sits on a bed that has a rug on it, breaks his oath. If he places another bed on top of it and sits on that, he does not break his oath. If he swears not to sleep on a mattress, and sleeps on it with a curtain over it, he breaks his oath. If he places another mattress on top of it, he does not break his oath.
20. Principle: These types are based on customary usage; so see what is intended in customary usage, even if the literal meaning is general.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings from the chapter on expiation (kaffarah) concerning oaths. The rulings focus on when an oath is considered broken (hanth), based on the literal and customary meanings of words. Key points: 1) Eating bread made from sworn wheat does not break an oath not to eat the wheat, because bread is a transformation. 2) Swearing not to speak to someone while they are conscious and hearing breaks the oath even if the speaker is asleep? The text says the person spoken to is asleep; if the speaker speaks and the hearer hears but is asleep, the oath is broken because the condition of hearing is met. 3) Agency: If one swears not to act (e.g. not to marry) and appoints an agent, the oath is broken because the act is attributed to the principal; but for transactions like sale, agency does not break the oath because the agent acts independently in the view of custom. 4) Sitting on a rug or mat is not sitting on the ground; sitting on a bed with a rug on it is still sitting on the bed. 5) The final principle states that all these rulings are based on customary usage ('urf), not literal dictionary meanings.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking of an oath; the legal consequence of doing the thing sworn not to do
محاورات (muhawaraat): Customary usage; the common understanding of words among the people
عرف (urf): Custom; the established practice of a community that shapes the meaning of words and actions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to eat flour, and then eats bread made from that flour, what is the ruling?
A) He breaks his oath
B) He does not break his oath
C) He breaks his oath only if he eats it raw
D) It depends on his intention

Key Takeaway
Customary usage (مدار على المحاورات) is the decisive factor in determining oath violations, overriding literal meanings.

Oath Conditioned on Ability: Health vs Power
Ruling that ability in an oath refers to physical health, not capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between istita'ah (health) and qudra (power) in Hanafi oath law and state the evidence from Surah Tawbah (9:42).

Core Idea
An oath conditioned on 'if able' binds only when health permits, not when one merely has capacity.

This passage from al-Quduri addresses a common scenario: a person swears an oath conditioned on ability. Does 'ability' mean physical fitness, or does it include any form of capacity? The Hanafi school, using a Quranic proof, rules that it refers specifically to health. Understanding this distinction is critical for correctly judging oath obligations and avoiding sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is swearing an oath conditioned on ability. The ruling is that the oath is binding only if the person is healthy (istita'ah al-sihha), not if they merely have general power (qudra).
2
Locate the dalil
The Quranic verse in Surah Tawbah (9:42) where hypocrites swore they would have gone out for jihad if they were able (istata'na). Allah exposed them as liars, indicating that ability here refers to physical health, not general capacity.
The verse is used as a textual proof (dalil naqli).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'istata'na' in the verse is interpreted by Hanafi scholars as meaning physical ability. The hypocrites' excuse was false because they were healthy; thus, istita'ah in oaths is tied to health, not to other forms of power.
The Hanafi usul principle: words in oaths are interpreted according to their customary usage (urf), and 'istita'ah' customarily means health.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some schools might interpret ability more broadly to include financial or other means. However, the Hanafi school is specific: istita'ah is health.
The thamarah al-khilaf: a healthy but poor person is still bound by the oath; a sick person is excused even if wealthy.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The mufta bihi position is that istita'ah means health, as established in Zahir al-Riwayah and affirmed by al-Quduri's matn.
This ruling is relied upon for fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَأْتِيَنهِ إِنْ اسْتَطَاعَ فَهُوَ عَلَى اسْتِطَاعَةِ الصَّحَّةِ دُونَ الْقُدْرَةِ
وجه (۱) الآية لثبوت وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَأْتِيَنهِ إِنْ اسْتَطَاعَ فَهُوَ عَلَى اسْتِطَاعَةِ الصَّحَّةِ \ وَسَيَحْلِفُونَ بِاللَّهِ لَوِ اسْتَطَعْنَا لَخَرَجْنَا مَعَكُمْ يُهْلِكُونَ أَنفُسَهُمْ وَاللَّهُ يَعْلَمُ إِنَّهُمْ لكَذِبُونَ (سورة التوبة، 9 أيت، نمبر 42)
And if he swears that he will certainly come to him if he is able, then it is upon the ability of health, not of power. Proof (1): The verse for the proof... and they will swear by Allah, if we were able we would have gone out with you...
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage deals with an oath conditioned on ability (istita'ah). The matn rules that 'ability' in such oaths refers to physical health and fitness (istita'at al-sihha), not to general power or capacity (qudra). The proof is from the Quranic verse in Surah Tawbah (9:42) where the hypocrites swore they would have joined jihad if they were able (istata'na). Allah exposed them as liars, indicating that their ability was based on health, not mere capacity. Thus, if one swears 'I will come if I am able', and he is healthy, he is bound to come; if he is ill, he is excused.

Key Terms
استطاعة (istita'ah): Ability; in this context, physical health and soundness of body.
قدرة (qudra): Power or capacity, which may include financial or other means, not just health.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Financial ability
C) Any capacity to act

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oath law, 'ability' means physical health, not general capacity.

Defining Vague Time Terms in Oaths: Hin, Zaman, and Dahr
Definition of vague time terms in oaths as six months

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default duration for hin, zaman, and dahr in Hanafi fiqh when no intention is specified, and identify the proofs for this ruling.

Core Idea
Hin in an oath means six months by default—the Hanafi school fixes the indefinite period to a legally certain duration.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses a common problem: when a person swears using a vague time term like 'hin' or 'zaman,' how long is the oath binding? The Hanafi school provides a clear default based on the saying of Ali (RA) and Quranic usage. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its proofs, and the role of intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath using vague time terms like hin, zaman, or dahr. The ruling is that the default duration is six months for hin, zaman, and dahr (according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
If the swearer had no specific intention, six months is the binding period.
2
Locate the proof for hin
The author cites the saying of Ali (RA) that 'al-hin is six months' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi) and a Quranic verse (Surah Ibrahim 14:25) where 'hin' is used for a period.
The qawl sahabi is zanni in transmission and meaning but serves as the primary evidence.
3
Understand the role of intention
The Urdu comment clarifies: if the swearer had no intention, six months is default; if he intended a longer or shorter period, his intention is followed.
Intention overrides the default but must be plausible (not contradictory to the term).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference on dahr
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad include dahr as six months; Abu Hanifa's view on dahr is not mentioned here but may differ (possibly longer).
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view as per Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Analyse the evidence strength
The qawl sahabi has zanni thubut and dalala; the Quranic verse supports the usage but is not definitive proof for a fixed duration. The Hanafi school uses istihsan to adopt six months as a practical default.
This shows how Hanafi fiqh balances textual evidence and practical necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُ فُلَانًا حِينًا أَوْ زَمَانًا أَوْ الحينَ أَو الزَّمَانَ فَهُوَ عَلَى سِتَّةِ أَشْهُرا وَكَذَلِكَ الدَّهْرُ عِنْدَ أَبِي يُوسُفَ وَمُحَمَّدٍ

لَعَلَّهُمْ
اصول : اگر حین بول کوئی نیت نہیں کی تو چھ ماہ مراد ہو گی اور اگر اس سے زائد کی نیت کی تو نیت ہی کا اعتبار
And if he swears that he will not speak to so-and-so for a hin, or a zaman, or the hin, or the zaman, then it is upon six months, and similarly dahr according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Proof (1): The saying of the Companion... 'al-hinu is six months'. Proof (2): The verse... 'it brings forth its fruit every hin' (Surah Ibrahim, 14:25). Principle: If no intention, then hin means six months; if he intended more, his intention is considered.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage clarifies vague time terms used in oaths. The matn states that 'hin', 'zaman', 'al-hin', 'al-zaman' all refer to a period of six months. The same applies to 'dahr' according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn). The first proof is the saying of Ali (radiyallahu anhu) that al-hin is six months. The second proof is from the Quranic verse in Surah Ibrahim where 'hin' is used for a period (interpreted as six months by some). The Urdu comment adds that if the swearer has no specific intention, the default is six months; if he intended a longer period, his intention is followed. This shows the importance of intention in determining ambiguous terms.

Key Terms
حين (hin): A period of time; in oaths, default six months.
زمان (zaman): Time; synonymous with hin in this context.
دهر (dahr): Era or long time; ruled as six months by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Six months

Key Takeaway
Vague time terms in Hanafi oaths default to six months unless the swearer's intention specifies otherwise, balancing textual evidence and practical certainty.

Days in Oaths: Ayyam and al-Ayyam
Dispute over meaning of indefinite days in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default meanings for 'ayyam' (three days) and 'al-ayyam' (disputed: ten vs. seven) in Hanafi oaths, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, 'ayyam' means three days; 'al-ayyam' means many days — Abu Hanifa says ten, Sahibayn say seven.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses the interpretation of two related terms in oath formulations. The indefinite plural 'ayyam' has a settled meaning of three days based on customary usage. The definite 'al-ayyam', however, sparks an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds it to be ten days, drawing on Quranic precedent, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it to be seven days (a week). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly assessing the duration of an oath and for issuing sound fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Identify 'ayyam' (indefinite plural 'days') and 'al-ayyam' (definite plural 'the days') in the passage. Note that the definite article changes the default meaning.
'Ayyam' occurs without the definite article; 'al-ayyam' includes it.
2
Extract the settled ruling for 'ayyam'
Find that 'ayyam' is unanimously interpreted as three days. This is a fixed rule in the Hanafi school.
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is not disputed.
3
Analyze the dispute over 'al-ayyam'
Note that for 'al-ayyam', there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. State Abu Hanifa's position (ten days) and the Sahibayn's position (seven days).
The Urdu gloss confirms: 'Imam Abu Hanifa: ten; Sahibayn: seven.'
4
Consider the thamarah al-khilaf
Determine the practical consequence: The duration of the oath changes. A person swearing by 'al-ayyam' must avoid the act for either ten or seven days, depending on which view is followed.
The mufti may choose either view based on context and leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُهُ أَيَّامًا فَهُوَ عَلَى ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ وَلَوْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُهُ الْأَيَّامَ فَهُوَ عَلَى عَشَرَةِ أَيَّامٍ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَعِنْدَهُمَا عَلَى أَيَّامٍ أُسْبُوعِ ه
اصول : ایاما کثیر اسے مراد دس یوم ہے امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک، اور صاحبین کی نزدیک سات یوم ہے۔
And if he swears he will not speak to him for ayyam (days), then it is upon three days. And if he swears he will not speak to him for al-ayyam (the days), then it is upon ten days according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (Sahibayn) upon the days of a week. Principle: Al-ayyam (many days) means ten days according to Abu Hanifa, and seven days according to the Sahibayn.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage covers two cases: indefinite 'ayyam' (days) and definite 'al-ayyam' (the days). For 'ayyam', the ruling is consistent: it means three days. For 'al-ayyam', which implies many days, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that 'al-ayyam' means ten days, drawing on Quranic usage (e.g., the story of Musa spending ten nights). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that 'al-ayyam' means seven days, i.e., a week. The Urdu comment reiterates this ikhtilaf. The mufta bihi position may vary; usually, the Sahibayn's view is considered more cautious in some contexts, but Abu Hanifa's is also relied upon.

Key Terms
أيام (ayyam): Indefinite days; ruled as three days.
الأيام (al-ayyam): Definite plural (the days); implies many days, disputed between ten and seven.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the duration intended by 'al-ayyam' in an oath?
A) Three days
B) Seven days
C) Ten days
D) One month

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi oaths, 'ayyam' defaults to three days, while 'al-ayyam' is disputed: Abu Hanifa holds ten, Sahibayn hold seven.

Duration of 'al-Shuhur' in Oaths: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Dispute over the meaning of 'al-shuhur' in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the meaning of 'al-shuhur' in oaths and identify the practical difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's interpretations.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'al-shuhur' means ten months (Abu Hanifa) or twelve months (Sahibayn) — the definite plural requires specification.

When a person swears not to speak to someone 'for the months' (al-shuhur), the duration depends on how the definite plural is interpreted. This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute on whether 'al-shuhur' refers to ten months or a full year. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply lexical principles in oath-making.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Oath and the Disputed Term
The oath is 'I will not speak to him al-shuhur'. The key term is the definite plural 'al-shuhur' (the months).
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Linguistic Basis
Abu Hanifa holds that 'al-shuhur' means ten months. He derives this from Quranic usage and the linguistic principle that the definite plural indicates a specific number in context.
The exact proof is not given in the text; this is a lexical ijtihad.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani interpret 'al-shuhur' as twelve months, i.e., a full year. They take the literal meaning of the definite plural as covering all months of the year.
4
Determine the Practical Consequence
According to Abu Hanifa, the oath is fulfilled after ten months; according to the Sahibayn, it is fulfilled only after twelve months. This affects when the oath is considered broken or released.
5
Identify the Mufta Bihi Position
The text does not specify the mufta bihi, but later Hanafi works may prefer the Sahibayn's view for caution or because it is the majority position within the school.
Check the 'mufta bihi' in the hanafi_position_box: both views are transmitted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يُكَلِّمُهُ الشُّهُورَ فَهُوَ عَلَى عَشَرَةِ أَشْهُرٍ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَعِنْدَهُمَا عَلَى اثْنَيْ عَشَرَ شَهْرًا
اصول : شہور سے مراد امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک دس ماہ ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک پورا سال ہے۔
And if he swears he will not speak to him al-shuhur (the months), then it is upon ten months according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (Sahibayn) upon twelve months. Principle: The meaning of shuhur is ten months according to Abu Hanifa, and a full year according to the Sahibayn.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage deals with the definite plural 'al-shuhur' (the months) in oaths. Similar to the previous dispute on days, Imam Abu Hanifa interprets 'al-shuhur' as meaning ten months, based on Quranic usage (e.g., the period of breastfeeding or other contexts). The Sahibayn interpret it as a full year (twelve months). The Urdu comment confirms this ikhtilaf. The practical effect is that if someone swears 'I will not speak to him for the months', his oath is fulfilled after ten months according to Abu Hanifa, but only after twelve months according to the Sahibayn. The mufta bihi position may vary; often the Sahibayn's view (twelve months) is considered more precautionary.

Key Terms
شهور (shuhur): Definite plural of month; in oaths, ten months (Abu Hanifa) or twelve months (Sahibayn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Oaths of Perpetuity and Single Action
Ruling on oaths to cease forever and to do once

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings for oaths of perpetuity and single action, and explain the proof from Surah Maryam 19:71.

Core Idea
A negative oath demands indefinite abstinence; a positive oath is satisfied by a single act.

Oaths are a serious matter in Islamic law. The Hanafi school distinguishes between oaths to abstain from something and oaths to do something. This passage from Al-Quduri’s matn lays down the fundamental rule: if you swear not to do something, you must avoid it forever; if you swear to do something, doing it once is enough. The proofs from the Quran and Sunnah reinforce this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of oaths
Recognize the negative oath (swearing not to do something) and the positive oath (swearing to do something). These are the two categories addressed in the source.
2
State the ruling for each type
For a negative oath, the swearer must abstain permanently. For a positive oath, performing the action once suffices to fulfill the oath.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
Examine Surah Maryam 19:71 which states that everyone will enter Hell; this is a decree determined (hatman maqdiyya). The Hanafi interpretation is that a single occurrence fulfills the decree, thus one action suffices for an oath.
Key phrase: 'hatman maqdiyya' indicates certainty and finality of the event happening once.
4
Analyze the hadith proofs
The hadith about children who die young shows that the Fire touches parents only briefly (one occurrence). The hadith of Abu Musa shows the Prophet breaking an oath for a better alternative, implying the original oath was fulfilled by one intended action.
These hadith support that one act is sufficient for positive oaths and that breaking a negative oath requires expiation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا حَلَفَ لَا يَفْعَلُ كَذَا تَرَكَهُ أَبَدًا وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَفْعَلَنَّ كَذَا فَفَعَلَهُ مَرَّةً وَاحِدَةً بَرَّ فِي يَمِينِهِ
د وجه: (1) الآية لثبوت وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَفْعَلَنَّ كَذَا ا ﴿وَإِن مِّنكُمْ إِلَّا وَارِدُهَا كَانَ عَلَى رَبِّكَ حَتْمًا مَّقْضِيَّا﴾ (سورة مريم، 19 أيت، نمبر 71)
وجه (۲) الحديث لثبوت وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَفْعَلَنَّ كَذَا \ عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «لَا يَموتُ لِأَحَدٍ مِنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ ثَلَاثَةٌ مِنَ الْوَلَدِ تَمَسُّهُ النَّارُ إِلَّا تَحِلَّةَ الْقَسَم.» ، (صحيح البخاري، بَابُ قَوْلِ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى {وَأَقْسَمُوا بِاللَّهِ جَهْدَ أَيْمَانِيمْ } ، غبر 6656)
وجه (۳) الحديث لثبوت وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَفْعَلَنَّ كَذَا \ كُنَّا عِنْدَ أَبي مُوسَى..... إِنِّي وَاللَّهِ إِنْ شَاءَ اللَّهُ لَا أَحْلِفُ عَلَى يَمِينِ، فَأَرَى غَيْرَهَا خَيْرًا مِنْهَا، إِلَّا أَتَيْتُ الَّذِي هُوَ خَيْرٌ وَتَخَلَّلْتُهَا»،(صحيح البخاري بَابُ الْكَفَّارَةِ قَبْلَ الْحِنْثِ وَبَعْدَهُ، نمبر 6721)
And if he swears that he will not do such-and-such, he leaves it forever. And if he swears that he will certainly do such-and-such, and he does it once, he fulfills his oath. Proof (1): The verse... 'There is none of you but will enter it (Hell), that is upon your Lord a decree determined' (Surah Maryam, 19:71). Proof (2): The hadith... from Abu Hurairah... 'Three children of Muslims who die...' Proof (3): The hadith... 'I do not swear an oath but that I see something better...'
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage gives two fundamental rules for oaths. First, if someone swears not to do something (e.g., 'I will never eat this food'), the oath implies perpetuity—he must abstain forever. Second, if someone swears to do something (e.g., 'I will give charity'), performing it once suffices to fulfill the oath. The proofs provided: the verse from Surah Maryam (19:71) about everyone entering Hell is used as an example of an oath that is fulfilled by occurrence (once). The hadith about children who die young indicates that an oath of punishment (Hellfire) is fulfilled by the mere entering (once). The third hadith from Abu Musa shows the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in handling oaths: if he saw something better, he would do that and expiate. This supports that one act is sufficient. The Hanafi principle is clear: for positive oaths, one action is enough; for negative oaths, the abstinence must be permanent.

Key Terms
أبدا (abadan): Forever; in a negative oath, it means perpetual abstinence.
بر (barr): Fulfilled the oath; acted righteously by doing what was sworn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears 'I will give charity', how many times must he give charity to fulfill the oath?
A) Once
B) Three times
C) Continuously
D) Only if he can afford it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a negative oath demands permanent avoidance, while a positive oath is satisfied by a single act, as proven by Quranic and hadith evidence.

Oath on Wife's Exit: Permission Wording Distinction
Difference between 'except with my permission' and 'except that I permit you'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two Arabic oath wordings 'illa bi-idhni' and 'illa an adhana' and apply the Hanafi ruling to determine whether an oath is broken.

Core Idea
The wording of an oath determines whether each action requires separate permission.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi fiqh scenario: a husband swears his wife will not go out except with his permission. The precise Arabic wording makes a significant legal difference. Understanding this difference requires parsing the grammatical structure of the conditional phrases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two Arabic phrases
Locate the two phrases in the source: 'illa bi-idhni' (except with my permission) and 'illa an adhana' (except that I permit you). Note that they appear in the same sentence structure but differ in grammatical form.
2
Parse the grammatical difference
In 'bi-idhni', 'bi' is a preposition attached to the noun 'idhni' (my permission), making permission a condition that accompanies each action. In 'an adhana', 'an' is a conjunction followed by a verb, treating the act of permitting as a single event.
3
State the Hanafi ruling for each phrase
For 'illa bi-idhni': each exit requires a separate permission; without it, the oath is broken (hanth). For 'illa an adhana': one permission suffices for all future exits; the oath is not broken by subsequent exits without new permission.
4
Apply to the given scenario
In the passage, the husband permits once. For the first phrase, a second exit without permission breaks the oath. For the second phrase, it does not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لا تَخْرُجُ امْرَأَتُهُ إِلَّا بِإِذْنِهِ فَأَذِنَ لَهَا مَرَّةً وَاحِدَةً فَخَرَجَتْ ثُمَّ خَرَجَتْ مَرَّةً أُخْرَى بِغَيْرِ إِذْنِهِ حَنِثَ أَوْ لَا بُدَّ مِنْ الْإِذْنِ فِي كُلِ خُرُوجِ وَإِنْ قَالَ إِلَّا أَنْ آذَنَ لَكَ فَأَذِنَ لَهَا مَرَّةً وَاحِدَةً فَخَرَجَتْ ثُمَّ خَرَجَتْ بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ بِغَيْرِ إِذْنِهِ لَمْ يَحْنَثْ
And whoever swears that his wife shall not go out except with his permission, and he permits her once and she goes out, then she goes out another time without his permission, he incurs sin (hanth) – rather, permission is required for every exit. And if he says 'except that I permit you', and he permits her once and she goes out, then she goes out after that without his permission, he does not incur sin.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two common wordings in oaths concerning a wife's exit. The first wording: 'You shall not go out except with my permission' (illa bi-idhni) means that each individual exit requires a separate permission. Therefore, if the husband permits her once and she goes out, and then she goes out again without a new permission, he has broken his oath and incurs sin (hanth). The second wording: 'You shall not go out except that I permit you' (illa an adhana laki) means that the husband's permission is a single act that suffices for multiple exits. So if he permits her once, she may go out repeatedly without further permission, and he does not break his oath. The key is the grammatical difference: 'bi-idhni' (with my permission) treats permission as a condition for each occurrence, while 'an adhana' (that I permit) treats the permission as a one-time authorization. This demonstrates the precision of Arabic in legal rulings.

Key Terms
إذن (idhn): Permission; in the first wording, it is required for each action; in the second, it is a one-time authorization.
حلف (halafa): To swear an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband swears 'You shall not go out except that I permit you' (illa an adhana laki) and then permits her once, does he break his oath if she goes out a second time without permission?
A) Yes, he breaks his oath
B) No, he does not break his oath
C) He breaks his oath only if he does not remind her
D) He breaks his oath if she goes out more than three times

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school meticulously distinguishes between 'except with my permission' (requiring permission for each act) and 'except that I permit you' (one permission suffices), based on Arabic syntax.

Defining Meal Times and Debt Periods in Oaths
Legal definitions of meal times and debt periods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definitions of ghada, asha, sahur, qarib, and ba'id, and apply them to resolve oath scenarios.

Core Idea
In oaths, 'lunch' is from dawn to noon, 'dinner' from noon to midnight, and 'near' means less than a month.

When a person swears regarding meals or debt deadlines, Islamic law provides specific definitions to remove ambiguity. This passage from al-Quduri establishes fixed time periods for common terms like ghada (lunch), asha (dinner), and sahur (pre-dawn meal), as well as default interpretations for 'near' and 'far' in debt repayment. These rulings ensure that oaths are enforced according to objective standards.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three meal times and their boundaries
The passage defines ghada from fajr to zuhr, asha from zuhr to midnight, and sahur from midnight to fajr. Note that these are meal times, not prayer times.
Sahur is the pre-dawn meal, distinct from 'isha prayer.
2
Apply the definition of ghada to an oath
If someone swears 'I will not eat ghada', the oath is broken if they eat any food during the period from dawn to zuhr with the intention of it being ghada. The content of the meal is irrelevant.
The oath refers to the time, not the type of food.
3
Apply definitions for asha and sahur
Similarly, asha covers the period from zuhr to midnight, and sahur from midnight to dawn. These apply even if the meal is eaten outside its customary time.
These definitions are default; if the swearer had a specific intention, that may override.
4
Interpret 'near' and 'far' for debt repayment
When swearing to pay a debt within a 'near' period, the default meaning is less than one month. 'Far' means more than one month. These are defaults when no specific period is intended.
The month boundary is a fixed benchmark in Hanafi law.
5
Recognize the importance of default definitions
These definitions prevent disputes and ensure consistency. They are applied unless a specific intention is proven that contradicts them.
If the swearer says 'I meant something else', the court will evaluate based on evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَتَغَدَّى فَالْغَدَاءُ هُوَ الْأَكْرُ مِنْ طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ إِلَى الظُّهْرِ وَالْعَشَاءُ مِنْ صَلَاةِ الظُّهْرِ إلَى نِصْفِ اللَّيْلِ وَالسَّحُورُ مِنْ نِصْفِ اللَّيْلِ إِلَى طُلُوعِ الْفَجْرِ وَإِنْ حَلَفَ لَيَقْضِيَنَّ دَيْنَهُ إِلَى قَرِيبٍ فَهُوَ دُونَ الشَّهْرِ وَإِنْ قَالَ إِلَى بَعِيدٍ فَهُوَ أَكْثَرُ مِنْ الشَّهْرِ
And if he swears that he will not eat ghada, then ghada is eating from dawn (fajr) to zuhr; and asha (evening meal) is from zuhr prayer to midnight; and sahur (pre-dawn meal) is from midnight to dawn. And if he swears that he will certainly pay his debt within a near (qarib) period, then it is less than a month; and if he says to a far (ba'id) period, then it is more than a month.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage provides fixed definitions for common time-related terms used in oaths. 'Ghada' (lunch) is defined as the meal taken between dawn (fajr) and zuhr (noon). 'Asha' (dinner) is from zuhr to midnight. 'Sahur' (pre-dawn meal) is from midnight to dawn. These definitions are important when someone swears not to eat a particular meal or to eat at a specific time. The second part deals with debt repayment: if one swears to pay a debt within a 'near' time, it means less than a month; if 'far', more than a month. These are default interpretations when no specific intention is expressed. Note: 'al-akr' is likely a typo for 'al-akl' (eating).

Key Terms
غداء (ghada): The midday meal; time from fajr to zuhr.
عشاء (asha): The evening meal; time from zuhr to midnight.
سحور (sahur): Pre-dawn meal; time from midnight to fajr.
قريب (qarib): Near; less than a month in oath context.
بعيد (ba'id): Far; more than a month.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, what is the defined time for the pre-dawn meal (sahur)?
A) Dawn to zuhr
B) Zuhr to midnight
C) Midnight to dawn
D) Fajr to sunrise

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides fixed time definitions for meal and debt terms in oaths to remove ambiguity and ensure consistent enforcement.

Oath Not to Reside: The Definition of Residence
Ruling on when an oath not to reside is broken

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when an oath not to reside in a house is broken, and identify the condition that determines residence.

Core Idea
Residence includes family and belongings; leaving alone does not break the oath.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from Al-Quduri: someone swears not to reside in a house but then leaves while his family and possessions remain. How should the oath be treated? The Hanafi school provides a precise definition of residence (sukn) that determines whether the oath is broken.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the oath
The person swears 'I will not reside in this house.' Then he physically leaves the house, but his family and belongings stay behind.
We need to determine if 'residence' ends when the person leaves alone.
2
Define 'residence' in Hanafi fiqh
In Hanafi law, residence (sukn) is established by the presence of one's family and possessions, not merely the individual's physical presence.
This is a key principle: sukn includes dependents and goods.
3
Apply the definition to the scenario
Since the family and belongings remain, the house is still considered his residence despite his personal absence.
4
Conclude: does the swearer incur sin?
Because he is still considered to be residing, his leaving alone does not fulfill the oath; thus, he has broken it and incurs sin (hanth).
He must therefore perform expiation (kaffarah).
5
Identify the condition for truly leaving residence
To genuinely cease residing, he must remove his family and possessions as well.
Only then would the oath not be broken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَسْكُنُ هَذِهِ الدَّارَ فَخَرَجَ مِنْهَا بِنَفْسِهِ وَتَرَكَ أَهْلَهُ وَمَتَاعَهُ فِيهَا حَنِثَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَيَصْعَدَنَّ السَّمَاءَ أَوْ لَيَقْلِبَنَّ هَذَا الْحَجَرَ ذَهَبًا انْعَقَدَتْ يَمِينُهُ وَحَنِثَ
And whoever swears that he will not reside in this house, and he leaves it by himself but leaves his family and belongings in it, he incurs sin (hanth). And whoever swears that he will certainly ascend the sky or turn this stone into gold, his oath is valid and he incurs sin.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses two scenarios. First, if someone swears not to reside in a particular house, but then he leaves the house himself while his family and belongings remain, his oath is considered broken because 'residence' (sukna) includes the presence of his dependents and goods; he is still considered a resident. Only if he removes his family and belongings would he be considered not residing. Second, if someone swears to do something that is physically impossible (like ascending to the sky without means, or turning a stone into gold by his own power), the oath is valid (yamin mun'aqida) and he incurs sin for failing to fulfill it, because he has made an oath to do something that cannot be done. He must expiate for breaking the oath. The Hanafi position is that oaths to do the impossible are valid; the swearer is considered sinful for making such an oath and must expiate.

Key Terms
سكن (sakana): To reside; includes living with family and belongings.
حنث (hanth): To incur sin by breaking a valid oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone swears not to reside in a house and then leaves, but his wife and children remain, what is the ruling?
A) The oath is broken; he must expiate.
B) The oath is not broken because he is not there.
C) The oath is broken only if he intended to reside with them.
D) The oath is invalid because residence is uncertain.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, residence (sukn) is defined by the presence of family and possessions, not just the oath-taker's physical presence.

Oaths to Do the Impossible: Valid and Binding
Ruling on oaths to do impossible acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an oath to do the impossible is valid in the Hanafi school and state the required expiation.

Core Idea
Impossible oaths are binding; the swearer incurs sin and must expiate.

This lesson addresses the Hanafi ruling on oaths that involve impossible acts, such as ascending to the sky or turning a stone into gold. Contrary to some assumptions, such oaths are considered valid (mun'aqida) and binding, and failure to fulfill them requires expiation (kaffarah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of oath
The oath involves an act that is physically impossible to perform through human power alone, e.g., ascending to the sky without means or turning a stone into gold.
2
Understand 'valid oath' (mun'aqid) in Hanafi
In Hanafi, a valid oath is one that fulfills the legal conditions (swearer, verb, etc.) even if the act is impossible. The oath is not void due to impossibility.
The key point: impossibility does not nullify the oath's validity.
3
Recognize the consequence: hanth
Since the act is impossible, the swearer inevitably fails to fulfill it, thus incurring sin (hanth) and must perform expiation.
The sin is for making an oath that cannot be kept, and the expiation is required.
4
Compare with possible oaths
If the act were possible, the swearer would be obliged to do it or else break the oath and expiate. Here, since it's impossible, hanth occurs immediately upon swearing? Actually, it occurs when he fails to do it, which is immediately because it's impossible.
The hanth is immediate because the act cannot be done, so he is considered to have broken it at the moment of swearing.
5
Differentiate from other schools if applicable
Some schools hold that impossible oaths are not valid; Hanafi is distinctive in upholding their validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَا يَسْكُنُ هَذِهِ الدَّارَ فَخَرَجَ مِنْهَا بِنَفْسِهِ وَتَرَكَ أَهْلَهُ وَمَتَاعَهُ فِيهَا حَنِثَ وَمَنْ حَلَفَ لَيَصْعَدَنَّ السَّمَاءَ أَوْ لَيَقْلِبَنَّ هَذَا الْحَجَرَ ذَهَبًا انْعَقَدَتْ يَمِينُهُ وَحَنِثَ
And whoever swears that he will not reside in this house, and he leaves it by himself but leaves his family and belongings in it, he incurs sin (hanth). And whoever swears that he will certainly ascend the sky or turn this stone into gold, his oath is valid and he incurs sin.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses two scenarios. First, if someone swears not to reside in a particular house, but then he leaves the house himself while his family and belongings remain, his oath is considered broken because 'residence' (sukna) includes the presence of his dependents and goods; he is still considered a resident. Only if he removes his family and belongings would he be considered not residing. Second, if someone swears to do something that is physically impossible (like ascending to the sky without means, or turning a stone into gold by his own power), the oath is valid (yamin mun'aqida) and he incurs sin for failing to fulfill it, because he has made an oath to do something that cannot be done. He must expiate for breaking the oath. The Hanafi position is that oaths to do the impossible are valid; the swearer is considered sinful for making such an oath and must expiate.

Key Terms
انعقاد اليمين (in'aqad al-yamin): The oath becomes valid and binding, even if the act is impossible.
حنث (hanth): To incur sin by breaking a valid oath, even if the act was impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The oath is valid only if the swearer believes it possible.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an oath to do the impossible is valid and binding; the swearer must expiate for breaking it.

Definition of Plaintiff and Defendant and Conditions for a Valid Claim
Defines legal parties and sets conditions for accepting a claim in court.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the plaintiff and defendant in Hanafi law, state the three conditions for a valid claim, and apply the hadith proof to distinguish plaintiff from defendant.

Core Idea
The plaintiff can abandon the suit; the defendant cannot. A claim must specify type and amount.

This lesson introduces the foundational definitions of the parties in a legal claim and the procedural requirements that make a claim acceptable in a Hanafi court. Understanding these definitions is critical because they determine who bears the burden of proof and how a claim must be articulated for the judge to proceed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Read the text's definitions: the plaintiff is one not compelled to continue the suit if he abandons it; the defendant is one compelled to respond. Note the opposite procedural standing.
The definition is about compulsion, not about who initiates.
2
Understand the procedural consequence
The plaintiff may drop the case at any time without penalty; the defendant must answer and cannot unilaterally end the suit. This shapes the burden of proof: plaintiff brings evidence or defendant takes oath.
Burden of proof follows from compulsion.
3
List the conditions for a valid claim
A claim must specify the thing claimed in type (jins) and amount (qadr). If it is a specific item (ayn) in defendant's possession, it must be identified by pointing or value. For immovable property (aqar), boundaries must be given and defendant's possession asserted. For a debt (haqq fi al-dhimmah), simply demand it.
Vague claims are rejected.
4
See the judge's procedure
Once the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant to respond. If the defendant confesses, judgment is given. No further proof needed. If he denies, plaintiff must produce evidence or defendant takes an oath.
Confession eliminates need for proof.
5
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr shows the Prophet treating the plaintiff as one who must bring evidence or else the defendant takes an oath. This indicates the plaintiff is not compelled (he can choose to bring evidence or abandon) and the defendant is compelled (must take oath if no evidence).
This hadith is ahad but relied upon in Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الدَّعْوَى
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ الْمُدَّعِي مَنْ لَا يُكْبَرُ عَلَى الْخُصُومَةِ إِذَا تَرَكَهَا وَالْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ مَنْ يُجْبَرُ عَلَيْهَا إِذَا تَرَكَهَا
وَلَا تُقْبَلُ الدَّعْوَى حَتَّى يُذَاكِرَ شَيْئًا مَعْلُومًا فِي جِنْسِهِ وَقَدْرِهِ فَإِنْ كَانَ عَيْنًا فِي يَدِ الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ كُلَّفَ إِحْضَارُهَا لِيُشِيرَ إِلَيْهَا بِالدَّعْوَى وَإِنْ لَمْ تَكُنْ حَاضِرَةً ذَكَرَ قِيمَتَهَا لَ وَإِذَا ادَّعَى عَقَارًا حَدَّدَهُ وَذَكَرَ أَنَّهُ فِي يَدِ الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ وَأَنَّهُ يُطَالِبُهُ بِهِ وَإِنْ كَانَ حَقًّا فِي الذِّمَّةِ ذَكَرَ أَنَّهُ يُطَالِبُهُ بِهِ
فَإِذَا صَحتْ الدَّعْوَى سَأَلَ الْقَاضِي الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ عَنْهَا فَإِنْ اعْتَرَفَ بِمَا قَضَى عَلَيْهِ بِمَا
The Book of Claims. He (al-Quduri) said - may Allah have mercy on him: The plaintiff is one who is not compelled to litigation if he abandons it, and the defendant is one who is compelled to it if he abandons it. A claim is not accepted until it mentions something known in its type and amount. If it is a specific item in the possession of the defendant, he is required to produce it so that he (the plaintiff) can point to it in his claim, and if it is not present, he mentions its value. And if he claims immovable property, he specifies it and states that it is in the possession of the defendant and that he is demanding it from him. And if it is a right in the liability (debt), he states that he demands it from him. Then when the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant about it; if he acknowledges (confesses), the judge passes judgment accordingly.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Claims (Kitab al-Da'wa) with essential definitions and procedural rules. The author first distinguishes the plaintiff from the defendant by their procedural standing: the plaintiff is never compelled to bring a suit—he may abandon it at any time—while the defendant is compelled to respond. This definition is used in the Hanafi school to determine who bears the burden of proof. A claim must specify the thing claimed in both type (jins) and amount (qadr). For a specific movable item (ayn) in the defendant's hands, the plaintiff must identify it by pointing; if absent, he states its value. For immovable property (aqar), he must describe its boundaries and assert the defendant's possession. For a debt (haqq fi al-dhimmah), he simply states the demand. Once the claim satisfies these conditions, the judge asks the defendant to respond. If the defendant confesses, judgment is given accordingly—no further proof is needed.

Key Terms
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The one who brings a claim; he is not compelled to continue the suit.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a 'alayh): The one against whom the claim is brought; he is compelled to respond.
الدعوى (al-da'wa): A legal claim that must specify the thing claimed in type and amount.
العقار (al-'aqar): Immovable property; must be described with boundaries in a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the Hanafi definition in al-Quduri, who is considered the plaintiff?
A) The one who must bring evidence
B) The one who is not compelled to litigation if he abandons it
C) The one who is compelled to respond in court
D) The one who has the item in his possession

Key Takeaway
The plaintiff is defined by his ability to abandon the suit; the defendant is compelled to respond, and a valid claim must specify the thing claimed in type and amount.

Procedure After a Valid Claim: Confession, Denial, Evidence, and Oath
Describes judge's response to confession or denial, and rules on evidence and oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for a valid claim, distinguish between confession (i'tiraf) and denial (inkar), and apply Abu Hanifah's rule on the plaintiff's request for oath when evidence is present.

Core Idea
Confession ends the case; denial shifts the burden of proof; evidence comes before oath.

In a court proceeding, once the claim is valid, the judge's next steps depend on the defendant's response. This passage from Al-Quduri outlines the sequential process: confession leads to immediate judgment, denial triggers the plaintiff's duty to produce evidence, and only if no evidence is presented does the defendant's oath become relevant. A key intra-Hanafi dispute arises when the plaintiff claims to have evidence but requests the oath instead. Abu Hanifah's ruling, which is the relied-upon position, prioritises evidence over oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defendant's response
Determine whether the defendant confesses (i'tiraf) or denies (inkar). This triggers two different branches of procedure.
2
If confession, note immediate judgment
Confession is conclusive evidence against the confessor. The judge gives judgment without further proof.
3
If denial, ask plaintiff for evidence
The burden of proof shifts to the plaintiff. The judge asks him to produce bayyinah (evidence).
Bayyinah typically means two male witnesses or one male and two females.
4
If no evidence and oath requested, administer oath
If the plaintiff is unable to produce evidence and requests the defendant's oath (yamin), the judge administers the oath to the defendant.
The oath is a last resort to decide the case.
5
Analyse the exception
If the plaintiff says 'I have evidence present' and asks for the oath, according to Abu Hanifah, the judge does not administer the oath but orders the plaintiff to present the evidence first.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ and allow the oath, but Abu Hanifah's view is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِذَا صَحتْ الدَّعْوَى سَأَلَ الْقَاضِي الْمُدَّعَى عَلَيْهِ عَنْهَا فَإِنْ اعْتَرَفَ بِمَا قَضَى عَلَيْهِ بِمَا وَإِنْ أَنْكَرَ سَأَلَ الْمُدَّعِي الْبَيِّنَةَ فَإِنْ أَحْضَرَهَا قَضَى بِمَا لا وَإِنْ عَجَزَ عَنْ ذَلِكَ وَطَلَبَ يَمِينُ خَصْمِهِ أَسْتَخْلِفَ عَلَيْهَا وَإِنْ قَالَ: لي بَيِّنَةٌ حَاضِرَةٌ وَطَلَبَ الْيَمِينَ لَمَّ يُسْتَحْلَفْ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ
Then when the claim is valid, the judge asks the defendant about it; if he acknowledges (confesses), the judge passes judgment accordingly. And if he denies, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence; if he produces it, the judge passes judgment accordingly. And if he is unable to do that and asks for the oath of his opponent, he is made to swear against him. And if he says: I have evidence present and asks for the oath, he is not made to swear according to Abu Hanifah.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage completes the initial court procedure. After a valid claim, the judge asks the defendant to respond. If the defendant confesses (i'tiraf), judgment is immediate—no further proof required, because acknowledgment is conclusive. If the defendant denies (inkar), the judge demands evidence from the plaintiff. This is based on the well-known hadith: 'البينة على المدعي واليمين على المدعى عليه' (evidence is upon the claimant, oath upon the denier). The Quranic verse (2:282) about witnesses also supports this. If the plaintiff cannot produce evidence, he may request that the defendant take an oath of denial. The judge then administers the oath to the defendant. However, Abu Hanifah states a key exception: if the plaintiff says 'I have evidence present here now' and simultaneously asks for the defendant's oath, the judge does not administer the oath. The reasoning is that the plaintiff's statement implies he could prove his case, but he is choosing an easier route, which is not allowed; he must first present his evidence. The other Hanafi imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ and allow the oath in this case, but the text here presents Abu Hanifah's view as the relied-upon position. The usul note at the bottom of the page confirms that after a confession, no oath is needed; judgment is given directly.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence; typically two male witnesses or one male and two females, sufficient to prove a claim.
اليمين (al-yamin): Oath taken by the defendant in denial; administered by the judge when plaintiff has no evidence.
الاعتراف (al-i'tiraf): Confession by the defendant; it suffices for judgment without further proof.
الإنكار (al-inkar): Denial by the defendant; triggers the plaintiff's obligation to produce evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what happens if the plaintiff says 'I have evidence present' and asks for the defendant's oath?
A) The judge administers the oath to the defendant
B) The judge asks the plaintiff to first present his evidence
C) The judge rejects the case
D) The judge asks the defendant to confess

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi court procedure, confession is dispositive, evidence is primary proof, and oath is a last resort; when evidence is available, oath is not permitted.
